RIOTERS IN STREETS OF BRUSSELS
CHARGED BY THE POLICE.
HURRYING GENDARMES FROM THE PROVINCES.
BRUSSELS, June 2S.â A disturb
ance occurred in the Chamber of
Deputies to-day when the order
of the day of the Right was
adopted, expressing confidence in
the president of the house against the
vote of Socialists. The announcement
of the vote was the signal for a general
uproar, all the Deputies rushing to the
center of the floor, where a free fight
took place. M. Guchtenacre, Catholic-
Labor member from Ghent, was badly
beaten by Socialists. A guard of sol
diers on duty finally cleared the gal
leries and the session was suspended.
The Minister of War. M. J. H. P. Van
den Peereboom, was the object of great
abuse by the Socialists, who blamed
him for originating the uproar.
BRUSSELS, June 29, 1 a. m.â At a
late hour demonstrators assembled in
the neighborhood of Parliament House,
the Government offices and the palace,
Cleveland and Toledo and across
Indiana to South Bend, and
thence to Chicago.
Leaving Chicago the route
will be across* the prairies to the
Mississippi River at Davenport,
and due west to Dcs Moines.
The ' Missouri River will be
crossed at Omaha, and the
course will be southwest through
Nebraska and on to Denver.
Mr. Davis expects to take the
pass near Boulder, Colo., and
work through the mountains,
going to the north of the (ireat
American Desert and turning
south after passing Palisade,
all of which were protected by the
police. The efforts of the latter and of
the civic guard to disperse the people
were fruitless. Finally a mounted body
of gendarmes repeatedly charged the
croud, greatly incensing the demon
strators, who hurled stones and other
missiles at the gendarmes, who there
upon discharged their revolvers. This
failed to scatter the crowd and the
gendarmes finally charged with fixed
bayonets and drawn swords. Two
gendarmes and several rioters were
wounded and many arrests were made.
Numerous windows and street lamps
were smashed and the scuffling con
tinued until after midnight, when. a
heavy fall of rain dispersed the demon
strators.
At the hour of filing this dispatch the
streets are still held by the police and
civil guards and detachments of the
gendarmes are arriving from the pro
vinces.

PACKING HOUSE STRIKES
CONSIDERED AT AN END
CHICAGO. June 28.â Wifh the exception
of the trouble at the Vilas & Robbing
plant, where about 500 men ami boys re
fused to work to-day, the epidemic of
strikes at the packing houses appears to
be at an end. In every plant notices have
been posted warning employes that any
one quitting work before the regular hour
would be discharged. The notices wco
the result of a conference held by the
packers on Tuesday and were effectual in
putting an end to the strikes.
Morris & Co. claim to be running aU
right, but despite the claims of th-- firm
thai it has replaced all its striking em
ployes, it has only been killing less than
half the usual number of hogs. The
strike center has jumped frcm the yards
to the plant of Vilas & Robbing at the
Halstead street bridge of the Chicag .
River. The killing gang and unskilled
laborprs went, out and operations j?er<
suspended. About 500 men and boys are
involved in the strike. They demand the
restoration of the 1593 scale.

Berkey of this county has announced his

cars of the larg". fruit shipping compa
nies, which, it is claimed, have heretofore
; ed taxation. In accordance with this
design, the Assessor this afternoon caused

j tary of the Continpntal Fruit Express.
: whom he subjected to a series of ques
tions relative to what numbers of re
frigerator cars of the company he repre
were located in this county on
March 1 last. Dewees said that the state
ment which he had previously rendered
the Assessor was correct, so far as he
knew. This statement made it appear
that the only property of the Continental
Fruit Express in Sa--rn.mento County on
March 1 last was as follows: Office furnl
; Hire, $7.'; safe and fixtures, $50: ice house
; $250; total, $875. He said he knew of no
way to o'etermine where the cars of his
company were on March 1. He said he did
not know whether or not his company
taxes in this State on its cars. He
; added that he did not know in what State
such taxes were paid, or whether they
were paid at all; that was outside his line
ol business.
After some further questioning, which
elicited no important information," the rep
resentative was excused. The agents of
! the other refrigerator car lines will be
called at different dates and similarly

â do not escape taxation. He says that he
j has learned that most of the fruit-grow
ers express. cars were in Los Angeles on

THE WEATHER KITE.
Alexander G. McAdie Congratulated
On His Experiments by Wash
ington Officials.
Alexander G. McAdie, chief of the local
Weather Bureau, received a congratula
tory message from Professor W.L. Moore,
chief of the Washington bureau, yester
day, on the result of his experiments with
kites to ascertain the temperature in up
per altitudes. From the weather station
op Mount Tamalpais, McAdie reached a
distance of nearly half a mile above sea
level. He found the temperature 30 de
grees warmer than in the city of San
I rancisco. At present he is perfecting
plans to continue his experiments.
Weather and crop reports were received
from Porto Rico by the local bureau yes
terday.
"Lightning and the Electricity of the
Air is the title of a recent work by
Alexander McAdie and Alfred J. Henry
Issued by the Department of Agriculture
and selling for 60 cents per copy. It con
tains valuable statistics on the effects of
lightning and its prevalence throughout
the United States. To-day the Weather
Bureau's monthly report will be issued
and will contain a table showing the rain
fail InjCalifornla for the past fifty years.
LITTLE BOYS WILD RIDE.
Thrown Thirty Feet Into a Creek,
But Escapes Injury.
MTLT. VAT. LEY, June 28.â When Frank
McCamlsh, a well known resident of this
place, drove into his yard yesterday he
thoughtlessly left his horse untied, not
noticing the presence of his 2-year-old
t."v. as soon as the father was out of
Bight the youngster climbed into the
and called to the horse, which
walked out of the gate. and. finding him
self uncontrolled, ran away. Tearing
down a steep hill at a high rate of speed",
tne horse overturned the buggy and threw
the child thirty feet Into h creek. The
buggy was smashed to pieces and the
animal badly cut and scratched before it
was caught, but the child miraculously
escaped serious injury and was merely
rendered unconscious by the force of his
terrible fall.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, JUNE 29. 1899.
CONFINED IN A
MEXICAN JAIL ON
A MURDER CHARGE
H. C. Faulkner, an Engineer,
Formerly of Redwood City,
Being Persecuted.
REDWOOD CITY. June 28.â A sensa
tional letter has Just been received by J.
j V. Swift of this place from H. C. Faulk
; ncr, who is confined in Jail at Ixtlahuaca.
Mexico. Mr. Faulkner has been confined
since April 25 last in a cell Bxlo feet, along
j with eight' other prisoners, the latter of
i the lowest type. The charge against him
is murder, and grew out of a most
lamentable accident, which occurred at
the Nolan mine at El Oro, where he was
employed as engineer, for which he was
entirely blameless.
It appears that the superintendent of
the mine together with an employe were
j let down a shaft in the mine in a cage,
their object being to take the water level.
Before going down the engineer was given
instructions to stop on signal.
The cage was lowered and no signal
I given to stop. Finally Faulkner thought,
! from the indicator,' that the cage must
be near the water, and of his own accord
stopped it. He received no signal even
then, and as It was against the rules to
raise the cage without orders from be
j low, he sent a man down to investigate,
who reported that the top of. the cage
was under water. The men had been
drowned like rats in a trap.
The letter, which was dated the lPth
inst., was smuggled out of prison and 13
as follows:
Dear Friend: You will no doubt be surprised
to hear that I am down here, and more than
that, I am In jail and unjustly held by the
authorities for an accident which occurred on
the 25th of last April at the Nolan mine at
El Oro, where I was employed. The casualty
resulted in the death of two men, and al
though I am entirely blameless, I have been
thrown into, prison at this place and have
since if confined In a cell not larger than
Bxlo with eight prisoners of the lowest type
j Imaginable. There is no ventilation and the
j stench of the place is terrible. My friends
i have done all they could to secure a trial for
I me, but have not yet succeeded, and it Eeenis
; all I can do is to Walt in silence in this hor
; rible place, for God knows when I will get out.
: The lood given us is not tit tor a dog, and
I what makes it harder still is that my health
' is about ruined from Bleeping on the damp ,
y stone flQgr, Ji -_.vfi\ili! jyu^Uje strongest, njej;.
' so you can imagine the cruelty iff. these Mexi
cans, especially when they have an American
!in their power. If the authorities would only
investigate the charges made against me they
; would at once see that I was powerless to pre
i vent the accident, or to save the lives of the
'â¢ men.
I will tell you briefly how the accident oc
curred, and every word of my statement can
i be proved by the most unimpeachable testimony
in the town of El Oro: I was running the
: hoisting engine at the Nolan mine, and on â¦he
i date mentioned the superintendent. Frank
Lloyd, stepped into the cage to descend, ac
: companied by a peon, to take the water level,
I giving me orders to lower -him until he sig
, naled me to stop. I let the cable out until
the cage had reached the 600-foot level and
' then slowed down to allow them to take the
bell rope. I still kept on paying out the cable,
expecting every moment to get the signal, but
no sign was given. The indicator showed that
! the cage had reached the end of the guides so
j I stopped and waited lor the signal, but re
! ceived none. I could not hoist the cage without
orders, so I Bent men down the main way of
the shaft to find out what was wrong, and
! when they returned they told me that the cage
was under water and both men were drowned.
My friends have appealed to the American
i Embassador at the City of Mexico, but he
| would do nothing for me. I have forwarded
i a statement to Secretary Hay at Washington
and hope he will Investigate the matter and
have my case investigated. I Intend to try
and make the Mexican Government pay for
their unjust and cruel treatment.
I write this letter principally to warn my
friends In Redwood who may think of com
ing to this country what they may expect
from such laws as exist here. I hope this
communication may reach you, yet I fear that
it never will. I have to depend upon the kind
ness of a prisoner who has the run of the
yard to address and mall it for me. I want
LEADS UP TO A
FORGERY CHARGE
Sensation Sprung in a
Petty Civil Case.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
REDDING, June 28.â A sensation was
sprung in the trial of a petty civil case
before Judge Cunningham at Balls terry
to-day. It will result in a prosecution for
forgery of W. Ford Spencer, a prominrnt
farmer, or his wife, or both. Suit was
brought by Mrs. Spencer in the Jutftic-'j
Court of Shlnpletown Township against
Joseph and Antone Martin to prove i
debt of $178, including a note for $25. The
Martins are widely known and respectc-d
sheep men. During the trial to-day
Colonel C. A. Garter, ex-United States
District Attorney, as attorney for the de
fendants, demanded to see the promis
sory note, denying I,he existence of the
samp. To the amazement of the defence
a note for $25, payable to Mrs. Spencer
and signed with the name, of Joseph
Martin, was produced. Martin was
dumfounded and stoutly denied the au
thorship of the note.
Judge Cunningham stopped the trial
and announced hi.s Intention of taking

MANILA, June 28.â One of the most
prominent Filipinos in Manila received
a verbal message this morning direct
from Aguinaldo, who is at Tarlac. in
structing him to make as complete a
report as possible regarding both the
military and political situation in Ma
nila, Aguinaldo wants to obtain from
a Filipino source knowledge concerning
the strength of the American army, the
health of the troops, and what Otis'
intent;,, 7>s ,:;-e regarding the carrying
out of operations u^rillg U\e rainy sea
son.
.The Filipino to whom the message
was sent has been steadfastly friendly
to the Americans. He has been fre
quently requested to join Aguinaldo,
but always refused to do so.
The fact that the rebel leader has
sent such a request is capable of dou
ble interpretation. Possibly Aguinaldo
desires to know what his chances
would be in the event of his surrender,
or it. is possible that he wants to obtain
information as to ports opened by or
der of General Otis, through which he
might obtain provisions and other sup
i plieÃ for his army and at the same time
' ship the produce of tne country.
The request for the information,
coming as it does direct from Agui
naldo, settles the reports that have
been circulated to the effect that he
has been assassinated by the friends of
General Luna, in revenge for the kill
| ing of the latter by some of Aguinaldo's
j men, presumably by his order.
Under General Otis' instructions the
! gunboats engaged in blockading duty
â¢ now allow vessels with regular clear
j ance papers to pass the blockade, but
; the issuing of an order to this effect
I was delayed, owing to the fact that the
j opening of ports would enable the in
surgents, who are in control of them, to
collect duties on articles imported.
There was great distress at several of
i the closed ports, owing to the lack if
! food, and business was at a complete
I standstill, owing to the merchants be
â  ing unable to obtain supplies.
These facts influenced General Otis
to issue the order, he considering that
I the situation is now sufficiently favor
j able to permit restricted business being
done at the ports. The order simply
permits the landing of food, and a clO3e
watch will be kept to prevent filibus
ters from landing arms, ammunition
and other supplies for rebels.
A severe typhoon is blowing arid this
is delaying the sailing of vessels which
â v^ant to take advantage of the opening
of the ports. The situation remains
comparatively quiet to the south and
east of Manila, where recent lighting
took place. A rebels' scouting party
to-day met a patrol of the Wyoming
regiment, and in seeking escape, fled
Into the arms of a battalion of the
Fourth Infantry. Two of the insur
gents were capturpd with their arms.
There is increasing activity among
j the Insurgents at San Fernando, and
i they apparently are anxious to resume
' hostilities. It is reported to-day that
j General Pio del Pilar, with 200 D men,
has joined the main body of the insur
gents there.

STOCKTON, June 2S.â Discord has
broken out among the exempt firemen

committee in inviting__ local Chinese to
contribute, a feature to the parade. The
local exempts are comprised of influential
citizens, and on parade they are always
one of the best features in a local pro
cession. Ben F. Kohlberg, president of
the company, announces that sinre the
Chinese have been asked to join in the
celebration and are preparing to do so
he wili fall a meeting of the company
and do ail in his power to keep the oid
vets out of lire.
"It think a man whp will turn, out with
a Chinaman is a Chinaman himself," says
Mr Kohlberg. "A few years ago we
were trying to get rid of the Chinese, and
now we are asking them, to turn out la
our patriotic celebrations."
Numbers of other exempts express
themselves the same way. The Fourth of
July committee Is left in a bad fix. It
does not like to notify the Chinese 'that
they cannot parade after extending them
a pressing invitation and can hardly daie
to offend the exempts. Diplomacy is be
ing tried, but it is doubtful whether it
â¢will work in this Instance.

FILIPINOS ACTIVE IN
SOUTHERN ISLANDS
VICTORIA, B. C, June 28.â The steam
ers Tartar and Glenogle arrived to-day
from Hongkong, bringing an interesting
story from Hakodate, where the steamer
Hokoku Maru had just returned from the
Philippines. Her master. Captain Kawa
mura Sakichi, reported that in the south
ern islands the young Filipinos are con
structing fortifications against emergen
cies. Every port is garrisoned by a thou
sand or so volunteers, whoge weapons,
however, are very crude, only about 20 per
cent being armed with rifles (Reming
tons). They are, however, full of pa
triotism and state that they will not yield
to the Americans, though the whole cf
the islands are destroyed. The photo
graphs of Aguinaldo are to be seen on the
walls of all the huts in the camps, and
his influence is great.
The Hokoku Alaru was warmly wel
comed by the Filipinos, who considered
the Japanese to be of a kindred race and
hoped for assistance from them. The
officers in the camps entertained the
ship's officers and crew, and endeavored
to make arrangements for a supply of
arms and ammunition from Japan, for
which they said they were prepared to
pay a large sum of money. They further
stated that they could supply large quan
tities of hemp as a return cargo for
Japanese vessels visiting the islands. The
Hokoku is believed to have done a prob
able trade in Remingtons and ammuni
tion, but her master naturally denies the
charge, asserting that all he sold were
two revolvers and the cook's knives.
There was no question but that the in
surgents are well supplied with gold coin
| shape of aluminum checks in denomina
j tions of 10. 25 and 50 cents, good for mer
chandise at the company's store. Saloon
Keepers and others- are forced to accept
these checks or lose trade. The checks
can be redeemed only by purchasing at
the company's store. Business men of
Keswick are complaining and are trying
to devise some means to obviate the ne
cessity of accepting the aluminum checks

swore to the complaint, says the orders
came from Constable John W. Kelly, his
chief The Board of Supervisors have
visited the theater on two different occa

pleased with the management of the

TO OWN THE CARS
Sense of the Placer^
County Convention.
ARGUMENTS ALL ONE WAY
NOTHING TO BE GAINED BY A
REDUCTION OF RATES.
Wells-Fargo Proposition Looked on
With Disfavor and a Private
Refrigerator Line Con
sidered Best.
facial Dispatch to The Call.
NEWCASTLE, June 28.â The fruit
growers met in Fraternal Hall at 3
i o'clock this afternoon. The attendance
was very small. C. H. Kellogg was ap
! pointed chairman and G. Geraldson see
, retary. W. R. Fountain suggested a
I rereading of the articles of incorpora
tion, stating that many growers were
: laboring under a false impression of the
sense of the Placer County association,
Bupposing that it did not favor an in
dependent car line. Mr. Fountain said:
"We wish it distinctly understood
that we do favor sucK a car line and
. will assist the State organization in
this regard. Although my proposition
was voted down in the State conven
tion I do not feel sore over it and am
â  willing to stand In on the majority re
port. Let me say, however, that if by
owning or leasing the refrigerator cars
every cent of th" refrigeration charges
were removed, the fruit growers would
secure one-tenth of one per cent
â¢>! it, but would exactly add to his
losses every dollar of the money he in
vested in the purchase or lease, main
tenance and operating expenses of the
1 refrigerator car line.
'Trace back and see if you can find
one instance wherein a reduction in
transportation charges brought the
growers any increase in their proceeds.
The trouble lies in having the other fel
low do your business. Fellow-growers,
do your own business and own a re
frigerator-car line and you will then be
the painer."
F. C. Miles referred to the actions of'
the State directors, and said that other
districts were prepared to follow Placer
and help the State association. After
considerable debate on what the future
actions would be, the proposition of
Wells, Fargo & Co. was mentioned by
J. F. Maden, but it was decided that
the proposition could not be properly
dealt with at present. An incorporation
was effected and W. R. Fountain, G. L.
Threlkel. C. H. Kellogg. C. L. Adams
and G. Geraldson were elected trustees.

COLUMBIA AGAIN
BEATS DEFENDER
Race From Brentons
Reef to New London.
SHOWS WINDWARD POINTS
ENTHUSIASTIC OVER EXHIBI
TION OF THE NEW BOAT.
The Weather Was Very Bad, but the
Columbia Floated Like a
Cork on Heavy
Seas.
Eueeial Dispatch to The Call.
NEW LONDON, June 2S.â ln a thir
ty-mile beat to windward to-day, the
new yacht Columbia heat th>' old cup
champion, Defender, eight minutes, be
sides giving her a two-minute handi
cap. Yachtsmen from New York and
hereabouts are jubilant, for the yacht's
Bterling qualitie must come out in a
race to windward. The race was from
Brentons Reef Lightship, off Newport,
to New London, a good thirty-eight
miles. The Defender was given a two
minute start. Thirty-two miles "f the
long stretch of water was a beat t"
windward, with bu< b a stiff breeze
blowing that neither yacht cared to
.any a working topsail, and it was in
this "kind ft' work that the Columbia
pained such a lons lead, averaging
nmn than a quarter of a minute faster
each mile than the Defender. Tha
water was very rough, but the new
boat rode very easily, and did not buffet
tlu- waves as much as the Defender.
A stifl breeze from the southwest kick
ed up quite a squall outside this morn
ing and put white crests on the waves
in tlu- inner harbor. The Defender
hoisted anchor shortly after 10 o'clock
and ran down the east passage under
her jib. Her mainsail was hoisted on
the way out and Captain Rhodes sawed
down as far as Castle Hill to get a look
at the weather outside and knocked
about the bay while awaiting the Co
lumbia.
The crew of the Columbia hauled
anchor at 11:15 o'clock, and she was
towed down as far as Castle Hill,
where the hawser was cast off and the
yacht beat out to sea under three lower
sails. The Defender was inside Bren
tons Reef lightship, loafing along under
a jib and mainsail waiting for her
rival. There was a pretty heavy sea,
and as the two yachts plunged along
clouds of spray were thrown into the
air from under their bows and fell
away to leeward. The weather was
rather threatening at the start and a
light rain compelled the crews of the
two yachts to don thir oilskins. The
showers were not heavy, but lasted
nearly all day. The Columbia ran up
to the Defender rapidly and continued
on the starboard tack until the light
ship was passed. The Columbia ' as at
that time 300 yards to the leeward of
the Defender and half her length
ahead. At 12 o'clock both boats tacked
around the lightship. They continued
along In this stretch for about five
minutes, the Columbia lufling up to
take a haul on the jib halyards. At
12 20 the Defender broke out her stuy
sall and the race was on, with the De
fender to the windward. At 12:30 both
ships tacked to port, heading toward
Point Judith and footing very fast. At
first the Defender gained a tritle, but
as they continued on this hitch the
Columbia crawled up slowly and but
f' r a shift of the wind more to the
south would have crossed the De
fender's bow on the next tack*.
ThTe shift in the wind helped the De
fender considerably and when at 1
o'clock th" Columbia tacked to star
board, about a mile off the Point Judith
whistling buoy, she could have almost
forced the Defender about, but the lat
ter tacked within a hundred feet of the
former under her lee bow. Captain
Barr could not stand having his wind
taken by the Defender long, so he
fetched about at 1:05 and stood up
along shore on the port tack. Then the
Columbia began to gain slowly on the
Defender and at the same time got
into the wind better. In twenty-five
minutes the new boat had worked
across the Defender's bow and was to
the windward of her. With plenty of
wind the Columbia and Defender held
down the shore on the port tack, the
former continuing to gain. Off Noyes
Point, at 2:20, the Columbia was over
half a mile ahead and steadily in
creased her lead and when she finally
came about off the end of Fishers Is
land, the distance between the two was
nearly a mile. At 3:2* the Columbia
took her final port hitch, this time
fetching well by Race Rock. The De
fender held on the starboard tack and
then turned in after the leader. The
boats proceeded up the harbor and
anchored.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, JUNE 29, 1899.
FERRY FIGHT
AT SAN PEDRO
C. P. Huntington on the
Losing Side.
DEFEATED AT EVERY TURN
SUCCESS WOULD KILL OTHER
ENTERPRISES.
People. Backed by Judge Stephens,
Appear to Hold Winning Hand
in the Struggle That la
Going On.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
LOS AXOELES, June 28.â The con
tention between the Terminal Raihvay
and the Southern Pacific Railroad that
is being fought out at San Pedro, that
In its eTSect seeks to prevent the Hunt-
Ington interests from obtaining a ferry
franchise and a foothold on Terminal
Island, this morning received a new
impetus in the hearing before the
Board of Supervisors. The seaport
town of San Pedro entered the arena
ami jumped with bewildering sudden
ii.ss into the very thick of the Im
broglio and made an unexpected slash
at the Huntington corporation that
proved disastrous to the octopus.
Judge A. M. Stephens, on behalf of
the people of San Pedro, led the cru
sa>!e. In his statements before the.
Supervisors the Judge referred to the
ii gal Features of the case and cited the
law to show that in the event of the
ferry franchise being granted another
terry could not be operated within a
mile above or below, which he asserted
in considering the topographical feat
ures of San Pedro harbor amounted to
handing over to the Southern Pacific
Company an absolute monopoly. He
said further that San Pedro wished to
i | crate a ferry across the bay and is
about to begin proceedings to condemn
a landing place on Southern Pacific
property. The granting of the proposed
franchise would, lie nUd, Interfere with
this project.
"This ag.-," Judge Stephens said, "is
supposed to be fighting monopolies, not
fostering them. This franchise would
absolutely exclude any other ferry,
even a free one. Another could not be
granted unless the grantee owned the
landing places."
Opposing counsel regarded the grant
ing of this franchise a,s one of a public
character.
"I do not," replied Stephens. "I
should not consider It public unless it
were open to all the public all the time.
I think that a ferry operated for the
benefit of the passengers of one par
ticular railroad could not be considered
as public."
The efforts of the San Pedro people
availed and thf hearing was set over
until Friday. So far in these many
contests for this franchise the Hunting- j
ton effort has at every stage been de
feated.

WILL FULLY TEST
SARANELI'S SERUM
Louisiana Health Board
to Use It.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
NEW YORK, June 28.â Through the !
agency of the Herald experiments of
world-wide interest will be made in this
country with Dr. Giuseppe Saraneli's
serum for prevention and cure of yel
low fever â experiments the usefulness
of -which are doubly great from the
standpoint of the United States now i
that it is necesary to keep American
troops in islands where yellow fever
may be a greater menace than a hos- i
Mle army.
The Herald received some days ago j
a cable dispatch from Count Malherbe, ;
Russian Consul at Montevideo, who !
has acquired Saraneli's serum, and
offered to the Herald 300 or more bot- I
ties of it to be used in experiments in :
Louisiana with a view to having its
properties subjected to a test under
proper auspices, and in hope that the
great claims made for the serum might
be justified and great benefit conferred
upon the world at large.
Realizing the importance of the mat- ;
ter the Herald at once accepted Count
Malherbe's offer and communicated in j
turn with the Louisiana State Board
of Health.
Dr. Edmond Souchon. president of j
that board, it is said, will accept the
serum and use it when an opportunity i
affords. He does not believe, however, !
that yellow fever is a great menace i
this summer, and that if the fever
does break out there may be a diffi- I
culty in properly testing the serum's I
efficiency, since he regards it as a pre- |
ventive only. Nothing would be
proved in case the patient to whom it \
was administered escaped the scourge. !
In view of the importance of losing no
chance to enter upon experiments I
which might prove of priceless value, I
he says the board for which he speaks :
will undertake the work.
When Surgeon General Wyman of i
Washington was interviewed by a i
Herald reporter regarding the use of
the Saraneli discovery as a protection \
against and cure for yellow fever he !
expressed great interest. As to the effi- !
cacy of the serum he does not feel at
liberty to speak in advance of the Yel
low Fever Commission appointed by
the President, consisting of two bac- i
teriologists of the Marine Hospital ser- I
vice, who have just completed their i
work and are now preparing their re- j
ports.

ALGER DIDN'T OWN
THE MATCH STOCK.
But He'd a Large Block as Se
curity, Which) Caused
a Lawsuit.
WASHINGTON, June 2S.â Secretary Aider's declaration that he
never owned a dollar of stock in the Diamond Match Company Is tech
nically correct, but that he was largely Interested in the company and
took an active part in its organizatfon is shown by the record of the
case of David M. Richardson against Christian H. Buhl and Russell
A. Alger, which was decided in thi* Michigan Supreme Court ten years
ago. Richardson brought suit to restrain Messrs. Buhl and Alger
from selling stock of the Diamond Match Company, held by them as se
curity. In the lower courts the defendants were directed to restore the
stock to Richardson and pay him a balance of $35,219 25. In the Su
preme Court the decree was reversed and the case dismissed. Chief Jus
tin- Sherwood, in announcing the decision of the court, recited a complete
history of the transactions which led up to the suit. He showed that In
1879 Richardson owned or controlled all the stock of the Richardson
Match Company, located in Detroit. Th<' plant had been idle for sixteen
months; The capital stock of the company was $75,000, divided into
3000 shares.

cover.

RIVAL BASEBALL
LEAGUE PROMISED
There Will Be Competition Next Year
at St. Louis, Chicago, New
York and Philadelphia.
.ST. INCUTS. June 2s.â The Post-Dispatch
to-day printed this: A well known hasp-
I ball man of St. Louis said this morning: i
I "A story comes from Chicago to the ef- j
] feet that a new association on th* lines I
of the American Association is to be
formed, the National League folks being; '
.in with the play. As a matter of fact,
the Chicago story is the first bluff pent
out by the National League to head off a
movement that has been on foot for some
â  time, and which means that the National
i League is to have a competitor in all its
leading cities. The National Iveague to
' day la a house divided against itself.
Freedman hates Brush, and Brush hates
Freedman, and Boden and Rogers have a I
; thorough contempt for Hart and the Rob- ]
insons, and the feeling is reciprocated by
; the latter trio.
"There is planning and scheming on all I
Fides, and out of i? next year will come a I
rival association to the present National
; league. This, in fact, will be the last j
year that any set of men will have a j
mortgage on the national game, and there j
i will be rival clubs, in every city in the i
j National League where the patronage will j
I warrant anything of the kind. Four cities j
certain to have competition next year are
St. Louis, Chicago, New York and Phila
delphia. |
I "The new association will have nothing
in common with the National League syn
dicate, and Its motto will be, 'Down with
; syndicate baseball and death to the slav
ish reserve rule.' In St. Louis the men â¢
back of the new association have already
secured grounds thirty-four blocks nearer
the center of the city than those now oc- i
cupied by the present St. Louis club."

of the list. The claim is raised that the

sence and hit it out at the start. Hardie

Regular Monthly Exhibition
Gives Promise of Usual
Exciting Sport.
The absence of the bookmakers and
other followers of the turf is noticeable
in the decreased attendance at the big
boxing meetings these summer months, i
This does not apply to the exhibition of
the San Francisco Athletic Club, as i
nvmth in and month out the number of
spectators is limited only by the capacity j
of th gymnasium on Sixth street.
The programme for to-night is miule up
! of five eight-round bouts, each pair of

|to prepare against nny misfits. Alex ;
I Greggains is the official referee. He has
incorporated in the Queensberry rules !
Hume ideas of his own which seem to
lpring out all the combativeness of the
boxers.

SAN FRANCISCO POLITICS.
HE new charter gives extraordinary power* to
"""I 'the Mayor of San Francisco. He will abso-
T" control f the School, Fire He Police de-
Intely control the S Fire 2nd Police de
: p.irtments. He can dictate from the selection oi
j school books, their use or exclusion, down to the pur
\ chase of fire hose and the assignment of beats to
â¢ roundsmen. . This city is naturally Republican. On
i a straight patty division we undoubtedly have a ma
â  iority. When a Republican candidate is of undoubted
I character and popularity he is usually reinforced by a
i large independent vote. When the whole Republi
! can ticket offers a decided preference to those who
i seek the best men to administer public affairs, it gets
! independent aid and succeeds.
The ' tor; of San Francisco proves that a man's
! politics and party connections do not afreet his char
â  acter and standing. A man is not honest because he
is a Republican, or Democrat, or Populist. The
bosses have habitually nominated bad tickets and have
aÂ«ked votes for them by proudly biting the party
I label. Mayor Phelan has personally selected a School
i Board and Supervisors, rounded them up and
i branded them with his crest and coat â â  ; arms, in al
dition to their party tap, and they proved the worst
....... has had out of a long line of bad ones.
The Republican party can win San Francisco, but it
must do it by nominating men who re something
more than Republicans. When their party skin is
scratched, ...... be found ber.eath it.
[ : The Call has stood by the Republican party in good
j report and ill, nd will be found abiding by Re; . -
! lican principles and to the front when and where they
; arc to be battled for. But The Call will not support
j a ticket in this city because it is called a Republican
' ticket. The men upon it must in their high character
| â¢ resent the great principles which they profess. II
i they do the party will win. If they do not. The Call
| will render the best sen-ice which can be given the
party, by defeating them. The Republican party is
stronger to-d because The Call ha; smitten, and
spared not, the tncKsters, ringstei and rascals who
seek to use the party as a train robber uses dynamite.
We propose thai its gain in character and in popu
lar confidence, by reason of its resistance to the cor
rupt forces which are always thin every, party seek
ing its control, shall not be lost by any retreat from
the position we have taken. If an honest Republican
ticket cannot be elected in this city none shall be.
We have no favorites to urge, no candidates to ad
vance for nomination. We only ask of those who will
â  direct affairs that they make a ticket that is above
\ suspicion. If they do this the party will come to its
i own in San Francisco. If they do not, the hungry
and dry Democracy will do some high looting, fcr
which the Republican party will be primarily respon
sib'e by us fa re to nominate good men. As lor us,
iif rascals must be elected they must be from the

HAWAIIAN '-GOOD GOVERNMENT."
AFK :ans in Hawaii are asking them
â  . _ ment" mÂ« v
â  â¢ ' {sessions. Two white laborers, bound
tract with a penal clause to labor on the
Honomu plantation, found tl subjected to
treatment as made negr - â  in this country
â¢ g and ran away. â  -as our s
used to depart by the underground railroad. They
were pursued, hunted down, and by a district magis
- â  â¢ â  until they would return to bondage.
They demanded a trial by jury, their right under the
constitution of the United States, and were refused,
and, when they appealed to the Hawaiian Supreme
; .una! anirrr.ed the court below and de
prived them of constitutional shelter by holding their
tary servitude legal, and they must either rot
in jail or go back to bondage. Remember that these
bondmen are white men. lured to the Paradise of the
Pacific to find it a hell.
The constitution of the United States say?:
"Neither slavery nor involuntary servitude shall exis:
within the United States or any place subject to their
jurisdiction."
By joint resolution of Congress Hawaii was an
nexed to the United States and made subject to their
jurisdiction nearly a year ago. The laws of the
United States forbid contract labor and the constitu
tion forbids involuntary servitude.
Since Hawaii became subject to our jurisdiction
about 30,000 Asiatic coolies have been imported to the
islands under contract, and some white laborers lured
there have been constrained to accept contract bond
age with the coolies. When Senator Morgan was
! iked by the native Hawaiians what they would get
for merging their sovereignty in the United States
and coming under our jurisdiction, he replied that
they would get '"good government.''
It appears, then, that good government in our dis
tant possessions consists in denying the right of trial
by jury and the enforcement of involuntary servitude!
Is it possible that the American spirit which was
roused by the return of negroes to bondage has so
completely died out that we ail consent to the return
of fugitive white men to slavery? When the militia
</. Massachusetts -,vcs called out to execute the writ
of the courts of that commonwealth and return the
negro, Anthony Burns, to slavery. Wendell Phillips,
addressing a great audience in Faneuil Hail, said:
"At the head of every judicial writ issued by a court
, in this State runs the legend, 'God bless the commou
vvealtfa of Massachusetts.' But if these writs are to be
1 used to return human beings to slavery, let the legend
be changed to read, 'God damn the commonwealth of
. Massachusetts." "
That climax roused the nation like a tocsin, and
; the current of feeling arose until, under the leadership
j of Lincoln, slavery was swept away and the constitu
: tien of the republic was made to canonize against in
voluntary servitude in any place under its jurisdiction.
The Call persistently warned the people of this
country of the results of Hawaiian annexation. When
â  President Dole said that after annexation "a way
would be found to get satisfactory labor for the plan
tations," we foresaw just what is happening now and
warned our readers of it. Unhappily, the most im
portant guarantees of the constitution, trial by jury
and prohibition of involuntary servitude, are abro
gated in the good government we have established
; there. An election being ordered for members of the
I local Legislature, the men who conspired for annexa
tion, being in fear of the reaction which appeared
among those who love liberty, induced President Mc-
Kinley to forbid such election. So we have another

A DAY AFTER THE FAIR
THE iixaminer, as is usual with that redoubtab'.:
:ate of "an internal policy/ 1 in the matt
- : the military excesses in the neighborhood of
tdner Idaho, is a "day after the fair."' In its
I :iay and Tuesday it devotes its fr
fa^re to the subject, which, in every conceivable a;- â¢
the amplest details, of fact, has been
- ; explained and is fully comprehended by
: the United States.
The attack upon property at Wardner by a number ]
of disaffected miners, supposed to be connected with j
the Miners* Union, occurred on April 29, 1899. . On j
May 3 The Call expressed the opinion that the law \
should be vindicated and the. guilty rioters punished j
on the ground that, in the United States, there is but;
one law for rich and poor alike. On May 14, under â 
the head of Military Imperialism," The Call, adher- ]
ing to its uniform position in favor of equality before .
the law. expressed the popular indignation bee. i
'under the pretext of suppressing riotous demonstra
tions, the Federal and the State constitutions." within
the r district around Wardner. in the county of Sho- j
shone, Idaho, had been "substantially trodden under
foot nd a practical illustration of imperialism fur
nished within the limits of an autonomous State.'' It
further brought to the notice of the authorities the
provisions of the Federal constitution relating to the 1
writ of habeas corpus, and, while relieving the Wash- |
ington ..... from ar.v possible complicity in
the intolerable despotism that had been temporarily
estabiished in Idaho, demanded its interference, to j
the greatest practicable extent, in order that the u?ual I
conditions mieht be re-established and the - rem
acy of the ordinary law of the State restored.
In these references The Call has no desire to vaunt
itself.' It simply performed its duty to the public, not
only in the expression of opinion but in supplying
the' news, not a single item of which of any mate
riality, in spite of the censorship of press dispatches,
has failed to reach it; columns.
There is also reason to believe that the administra
tion u a? performed its constitutional obligations, thai
I the military situation has been gradually ameliorated
and. that even the State authorities have been made
to comprehend that there are limits they must not
pas;.
After all this work has been done and these re
sults achieved through an independent and enterpris
ing press, voicing the true sentiments of American
! citizenship, the fossilized Examiner has discovered
! that there are a few questions to be settled in the
neighborhood of Wardner .-. I has twice devoted its
first page to a rehash of facts which every one has
I known for weeks. Indeed it is now probable that the
, redress of the grievances of various sorts that have
been so glaring in Idaho for nearly two months has
become part of the "internal policy of the organ of
Bourbonized fusion that has recently discovered the
illegitimacy of the political abortion it produced last
year. The ordinary reporters of the press having
completely gleaned the Idaho field and now simply
. fun -shine the fresh news of each day. a "commis
sioner" haÂ« been dispatched who has succeeded, after
much labor, in verifying the historical records con
tained in the columns of The Call, and, it may be
added, a large part of them original and exclusive.
Examiner, i
had any. is about gone. Its relation to the world of
rapidly accumulating news seems to be also in pr
of dissolution.
OUR HOME-COMING VOLUNTEERS.
SENATOR PERKINS' dispatch to the Presi
dent requesting information as to the
when the First California Regiment would em- :
j bark to return home has not brought forth any deft
nite information on the subject. Adjutant General ;
Corbin, replying for the President at the instance of ,
j the Secretary of War. does no more than direct the j
â  attention of the Senator to that portion of a recently
published cable from General Otis in which it is
' stated: "Nebraska. Pennsylvania and Utah now I
'taking transports, and Sixth Infantry sent to Negros
to relieve California."
From this information it appears the work of re
' lieving the volunteers is going forward about as rap
idly as can be effected under the circumstances. The
California regiment, however, instead of being first.
or among the first, to be relieved, is to be one of the .
hlast The Oregon volunteers are already on their way ;
; home, those of Nebraska. Pennsylvania and Utah
were embarking at the time Otis sent his cable, but '
the Californians were still at Negros waiting for the
Sixth Infantry to relieve them.
While the delay in the home-coming of our vol
tecrs is not according to our hopes, it is still one :
; which the people of the State will bear with the loy- ,
: alty of patriotism. It was a high compliment to the
Californians that they were intrusted with a separate
duty in distant islands far removed from the main
, command. They won that honor by distinguished
service and discipline, and the State, has reason to be i
â  proud of it. That separate command, however, de
| lays them at the front while others come home. High
. duties impose high responsibilities, and honors, like '
; everything else, must be balanced in the scale of com- j
: pensations.
It is now assured, however, that in a time compara
tively short our toys will be sailing home. Then will ;
! come an occasion for San Francisco to make a gala |
i day and invite all Californians to come to town and |
help in the grand reception of the home-coming he- j
j roes. That day is now not distant. Once relieved at
! Negros the volunteers may embark immediately for j
j home, and it is. therefore, none too early to begin j
â  preparations for making their welcome a festival of !
more than ordinary brilliance and enthusiasm.
Professor Gayley says apologetically that the Uni- i
verity of California is suffering from growing pains. \
'. Is it possible that the learned gentleman would wish
' to see the institution in the agonies of the rheumatism ;
i of old age?
So far friends of the British royal family have been â¢
able to keep from the poet laureate the knowledge \
that her Majesty ha? entered on the sixty-third year \
,of her reign. They fear it might provoke him to in- |
flict the public with one of his poems.
The other day a Columbus centenarian was di
vorced and was then almost kidnaped. He probably
thinks that he escaped two phases of the same fate on 'â 
the same day.
It is stated as a fact not to be doubted that there are
fortunes in oil in California. None of the painters
hereabouts seem to have found them.
SUGGEST OF OF NAP AS MAYOR
FOR THE DEWEY MONUMENT
MAYOR I. A. FULLER OF NAPA.
IF anybody thinks that Napa win lag behind her sister cities of
Si â  'n aiding the I - â¢:â  â¢ eminent let him, or her, read
the . oylng lettei ft Mayor I- A. Fuller:
NAPA. June 28.
To the Editor of The Call â Sir: In erecting a monument to
Admiral Dewey and the American navy, the people of Califor
nia have adopted the most worthy means of perpetuating his
memory. When a man reaches the pinnacle of fame it is natural
that his countrymen should seek some such means of honoring
him. It is my hope that this tribute to the great sea captain
â¢will surpass that erected by the English people to Lord Nelson,
both in proportions of architecture and elaborate design, as the
battle of Manila Bay surpasses the battle of Trafalgar.
Other States may follow the footsteps of California, hut I
want to see our own State outstrip all the others, and I would
suggest that every county in the State not only subscribe to
the fund, but also contribute a block of its most durable mate
rial to be used in the construction of this tribute of our appre
ciation of the matchless courage of this man. whose incentive
was fealty to his flag, whose hope of reward was the approval
of his conscience and of his countrymen.
I. A. FULLER. Mayor.
LAST OF THE
FOURTH SAILS
FOB MANILA
Presidio Changing Its
Garrison.
Another installment of troops went j
from the Presidio yesterday on their way
to the Philippines. The headquarters,
band and Troops B and M .of the Fourth j
Cavalry and Companies E and H of the â 
Twenty-fourth Infantry made up the de- :
tachment. The whole was under com- j
mand of Major Morton of the Fourth :
Cavalry. Companies E and H are new
arrivals at the post, but the two cavalry
troops had been there for a lons time. :
As a consequence when they started lor
the McDowell, in which they were taken j
to the Valencia, there were scenes very
similar to those which made the going
away of the First Infantry such Â«i
memorable event.
All departments at the Presidio are
bending their energies toward preparing
for the reception of troops, both coming
from and going to the Philippines. The
last of the Fourth Cavalry has gone. and
it will not be long before the Twenty
fourth Infantry will A>e away, but
there is no diminution in the num
ber of men quartered at the post. j
They are arriving by hundreds, and
the "parade ground is filled with drill
ing recruits every morning and after
noon. Tents are new being erected to re- i
ceive the Oregon Volunteers when ifcey
come, but if space is needed before tnat
time the tents will be used for recruits. It
is expected that the permanent canps
will be able to accommodate ten or twelve ;
thousand men when they have all been
finished.
Colonel Freeman, commanding the
Twenty-fourth Infantry, was expected to
sail for Manila on the Pennsylvania; then
he was changed to the Valencia, and
now it looks as if he would have
two or three weeks more here. It is
probable that he will remain as long
as there is any of his regiment left and
that he will go out with the last of it.
In the meantime who his successor as
commander of the post will be is a mat
ter of conjecture.
It seems to be the intention of the Gov
ernment to make of the Presidio a cav
alry or an artillery post, as it was before
the" war. Two troops of the Sixth. Cay- '
airy came in last Monday and more are
expected, while the Third Artillery, the
old garrison regiment, seems to be drift- .
ing back again. Light Battery C is al- !
ready there, and there/ is a detachment
of Battery B at Yosemite and another at
Alcatraz. The detachment at Yosemit?
will be relieved by Troop F of the Sixth
Cavalry within a short time.
Lieutenant Thomas F. Slavens has been
ordered to duty as quartermaster and
commissary on the freighter Weyfield,
which will take down the horses for the
cavalry men Â» going on the Valencia. A
non-commissioned officer and ten troopers
from each troop have been ! detailed to
care for the horses.
Company X of the Twenty-fourth In
fantry is reported from Assinaboine as;
having started for the Presidio. It will :
probably arrive to-day.
Colonel Cool'.dge has been changed from
the Valencia " and will go out on the
Pennsylvania â¢ . â¢ 'v=i>-'v" :
Lieutenant Lincoln Kilbourne, a sun-in
law of Colonel Evan Miles, who was for
merly in command of the pott, reported '
for duty at the Presidio yesterday. He
has been assigned to the casual detach
ment. â¢ . 'â¢- ' *
Dr. Cutter has ber-n ordered to duty at
Alcatraz to relieve Dr. -Dudley, who has
been ordered to duty at the general hos
pital. V .
Admiral Kautz. Lieutenant Miller, .flag
lieutenant, and Ensign Sellers, flat; sec
retary, and Captain White, commanding
the Philadelphia, made an official visit.
to General. Shatter yesterday morning.

ty-fifth- Infantry, - has been - appointed
mustering-out officer at San Francisco for
the. volunteers returning from the Philip-!

WARDEN AGUIRRE
BUMPS INTO THE
PRISON BOARD
A Clash Over Certain
Appointments.
From the common talk indulged in by
politicians,- it would seem, that Martin G.
Aguirre, the new Warden of San Qui
Prison, Intends trying things all
his own way as regards the matter of
appointments and conduct of the prison
generally. If what is said by several who
are in a position to know whereof they
sirak is to be relied upon. Aguirre has
already clashed with the Board of State
Prison Directors and will r.c: :' :
take his seat as Warden until July L'
The inception of the trouble between the
board and the new Warden, it is said, is
due wjiolly to an attempt on the part of
Aguirre, acting as the agent of Dan
Burns, to place ex-Supervisor Ed Smith
in the position of commissary at the
prison. This was the original intention,
but when Aguirre made known his desire
to make Smith commissary be m< I
decided opposition. The Directors voiced
their unanimous sentiment thai for vari
ous reasons Smith was
office and should never receive UK
dorsement.
Finding that argument and other de
vices were unable to gain his end. Aguirre
finally declared that he would appoint
Smith whether the board liked it or
But when he thus laid down his hand in
perfect confidence Aguirre found that ht
had been euchred. He was not politician
enough to cope with the veteran board
and was soon made to realize ::. Wli
a moment's hesitation he was informed
that as Warden he had a right to appoint
whomever he might wish, but that the
board controlled the salary of the posi
tion, and if Smith was appointed would
reduce it to $25 a month. Staggered at
this reply, Aguirre came to this city and
held a consultation with his political" mas
ters, the result of which was that it was
finally admitted the board held the whip
hand and must bo respected. So Smith
was not appointed.
But Aguirre felt so angry and chagrin
ed over his defeat that he immediately
laid plans to be revenged. He did not
have to nurse his wrath long, for a short
time afterward the board requested
Aguirre to retain Captain J. F. Biriem as
captain of the guard, and Indorsed him
as perhaps the most efficient officer at
San Quentin. Aguirre sweetly acquh -
but winked the other eye, at the same
time leading the board to believe that
Thomas Wilkinson should remain as com
missary. In fact, it is stated that the Di
rectora felt so positive of the retention of
these two men that President Fitzgerald
sent Wilkinson a telegram no later than
last Tuesday telling him to be at his ease
â everything had been fixed nicely.
On the same day Aguirre introduced ex-
Chief of Police Russell of San Diego to
Biriem as his successor and notified Wil
kinson that on the first of the month
Thomas Foley. the father of Gage's pri
vate secretary, would be made commis
sary. To complete his pleasant work the
wiseacres say. Deputy Sheriff McCiure of
Los Angelt-s was appointed turnkey to
succeed J. C. Jamieson. and Deputy Sher
iff Barnhill of the same place front gate
keeper to succeed Fred Woods. Captain
of the Yard Edgar, generally admitted to
be the most unpopular official at San
Quentin. has been retained for the sime
reason, but it is believed that even for the
purpose of revenge Aguirre cannot afford
to keep Edgar long.
The feeling is represented to be so keen
that the board will not let a long time
elapse before it will endeavor to make the
Warden realize that it is a large factor In
prison affairs and must be respected.

k regulator of tfce stomach asi bowel*. . . â  â¢WIFE KILLS HUSBAND
IN DAUGHTER'S SIGHT
Mrs. Kopp Sends a Bullet Into the
Brain of the Man Who
Scorned Her Love.
After Making Sure That He Was With Another Woman,
She Taxed Him With Infidelity and He
Laughed in Her Face.
CHARLES KOPP, a retired res
taurant keeper, was shot and
killed last night by his wife", Mrs
Louise Kopp, in the presence of
their 14-year-old daughter. The shoot-
Ing took place in the parlor of the fam
ily residence, 18 Lewis place, off Tay
lor street, between Post and Putter.
Leaving the body where it fell, blood
oozing from the death wound over the
left eye, mother and daughter left the
house, locking the door on their dead.
They visited the house of near relatives
in the Mission, informed them of the
tragedy and returned to their own
home, where Mrs. Kopp surrendered
â herself to the police.
"I did it," she exclaimed, dry eyed,
"because I wanted him for myself."
That short sentence told the whole
story. Kopp loved another than his
wife.. Jealousy seized the neglected
;cyo man./ already tottering to the grave
with consumption. She taxed him
with 1. .- infidelity, and he boastingly
ridmatted^t.- because she wanted
him for. herself, she shot him dead.

Under the name of Virginia Drew and
unknown to her husband Mr?. Trescott.
wife of Charles B. Trescott, the milllon
' aire fish packer of Portland, Or., made
| Jier debut on Monday evening at the Al
; cazar.
Mrs. Trescott has been In the city for
the past six months, presumably for her
health, but in reality giving all her time
and attention to fitting herself for the
Stage. No one about the theater nor any
of the people the lady has met during her
stay here had any idea that the woman
j who was making so many sacrifices in
; art's name was the wife of a millionaire.
She made no use of her social position to
help hrr in her artistic career, preferring
to stand on her own merits.
On Monday evening a well-known Port
land gentleman in the audience recognized
Miss Virginia Drew as Mrs. Trescott, and
yesterday the escapade of the mlllion
. aire's wife was the sole theme of gossip
both in social and theatrical circles.
â \Vhon Mrs. Trescott was confronted
1 with thfÂ» fact that her little romance was
known she frankly acknowledged that
rumor was correct and that she had left
I ease and luxury for art.
The new debutante, who has not created
I an unfavorable impression and who has
i shown unmistakable signs of considerable
kill us. Wo waited around till he had
been led away. Since then he has not
been at the house, and I am afraid
ho will kill us unless he is arrested
and sent to jail.
"We will get married, notwithstand
ing his threats, but it is annoying to
have him around with a loaded re
volver in his ham! to shoot us."
The warrant was issued and given
into the hand? of an officer, but up to
a late hour last night he had not been
able to find Whittle.
talent In the line shp has chosen, told in
tragic tone? how "art wooed and won"
her and how in the future she intended to
give her whole life to- the stage.
"Since 1 was 16," said Mrs. Treseott, "I
have felt the desire to go upon the stage.
It Is in my blood. My mother was a
Drew. Mr. TreÃCOtt ami I were not con- j
genial. He does nothing but sleep and
Count his money. I long for an artistic \
atmosphere. I
"When I cime to town last winter," the
lady continued. "I decided to prepare my
self for the stage. I had been studying
for years to thai end, but lacked the tech
nical training. I went through a most
rigid course. I took up psychical re
search, theosophy, Christian Science and
hypnotism with the intention of learning
to establish an electrical current between
myself and my audience and thereby
draw them toward me.
"Mr. Trescotl made me an allowance â a I
very small one when considered In pro- j
portion to his vast wealthâ but my studies
came high, and many times the taking of
a lesson has meant for me privation."
Mrs. Trescott explained that she had
not told her husband of her Intentions in
regard to a theatrical career, for she :
knew he would not approve of it and that
his disapproval would probably take the
inconvenient form of a failure to send !
remittances. "When I won fame, as I 1

the would-be debutante. "He Is a sure- ;
thing man. and if he -saw I was a winner I
he would hank on me."
Mr.--. Trescott has the physique critics
are pleased to describe as that of a trag
edy queen. She pays she longs to play
Berntfardt roles ;ind that the glove and
boot that fit the divine Sarah are of the
size she herself wears.
"Emotional is my forte." said Mrs.
Trcsoott. "In that way I feel I can pive
vent to all the suffering I have endured
for years."
Mrs. Trescott is well known In Port
land, Or., and has for many years been
a recognized social leader there. She is
a Southern woman of excellent family
and was wedded to Trescott six years ago.
Her uncle. Ethan Allen, is one of the mil
lionaire merchants of Portland.

THE DIRECTORS
HOLD ANOTHER
SECRET SESSION
Discussing the Text
books.
NOT MANY CHANGES MADE
HEAD WAS NOT CONSULTED ON
JANITORS.
He Made Complaint and an Interest
ing Report Was Referred
Back for Consid
eration.
The Board of Education hid its face
agrain last evening and discussed text
hooks behind closed doors. Textbooks
were what caused a good part of the
scandal in the administration of the last
board. The executive session was not
called without a protest from Director
Head, however, but the board preferred
secrecy and sought it in adjoining
room.
When the motion to discuss the text
books in committee of the whole was
made by Stow, Head grew suspicious and
demanded to know whether the conven
tion of the committee of the whole would
be secret. President Bergerot said he
could not guess at the intentions of the
board. Head declared the subject of text
books was a most important one, and one
in which every parent had an interest,
and he believed the matter should be dis
cussed in public. The motion to go into
committee of the whole prevailed, how
ever. Head was called to the chair, and
then Bergerot himself made the motion
to go into executive session.
Head got in another protect on the re
port of the committee on janitors. Head
is a member of the committee, and when
he found a report had been submitted he
got up and declared there had been no
meeting of the committee, for he had
never been notified. The names of Cole
and Kemp were signed to the report, but
Cole, the committee chairman, was forced
to admit that he had called no meeting,
and so the report was sent back to the
committee until it could be presented in
proper form. The report was as follows:
Your Committee on Janitors respectfully
recommends the following chÃn*es: Mrs. Kate
Halnes. vice Mrs. J. M. Murphy, Bernal
School- Mrs. M. McClellan, vice Mrs. J. Hun

Jack-Ron, vice J. O'Farrell, Horace Mann
School.

HOLIDAY IN STOCK EXCHANGE MONDAY.
NEW YORK. June 2S.â The governors of the
New York Stock Exchange have decided to sus
pend business Monday, July 3. Their action
permits a holiday for members from Saturday
noon until Wednesday at 10 o'clock.

CHICAGO GRAIN MARKET.
CHICAGO. June 28.â Conditions at the open
ing In Wheat favored higher prices. Liver-
I pool showed a liberal response to yesterday's
! advance here, opening V'<-V; higher and main
tained most of its advance. Continental mar
kets were also higher. A decidedly bullish
feature was- the falling off in Northwest re
ceipts and influenced the market enough to !
keep prices >n the upgrade for about half an
hour alter the opening. Then the weakness
at St. Louts which wan thought to be due to !
expectations of larger receipts of new Wheat '
in the near future and weekly crop reports
started a moderate felling movement which
carried prices considerably below the opening
point and gave the old familiar appearance or
weakness to things in general. September
; opened at "i'^fi'^c. compared with yester
day's closing price of 74^c. It advanced to
74\'</74\i . then gradually slid off until Tl I*'1 *'
had been reached. At this point the market
took another turn and until within about half
an hour 'of the close, though extremely quiet.
1 was strong. . St. Louis' weakness was said to
be due entirely to local elevator difficulties'
and not to fear of heavy new Wheat receipts.
Crop advices from all sections of the spring
Wheat country told of damage from rust and
from wet weather followed by the heat of last
Sunday. The Illinois State report estimated ;
that 1 per cent of the crop was lost and the ;
condition of the rest only 38 per cent. Sep- !
temper advanced to 74V"""<\ when a realizing
movement set in which carried September to
. 74V. where It closed.
: Corn was dull and heavy. The market started
with Wheat, but liberal receipts, favorable
crop conditions and heavy estimated receipts
to-morrow started liquidation. September
closed %'s>-ic lower.
Cats were dull but firm. September closed
with a gain of %c. Crop damage reports and
foreign advices were the bullish features.
Provisions were strong. Hog prices were '<
higher and this with the strength of Wheat
and the good shipping demand kept prices firm
and a little higher. At the close September
Pork was 5Â©7Â«4c higher. Lard 5c higher and
Ribs 2V''>c higher.

WHEAT AND OTHER GRAINS.
WHEATâ The Chicago market opened firmer
on better foreign advices. The Northwestern
reports were less favorable for the crops, late
rains having done considerable damage. Tlio
Illinois State report placed the yield of that
State at 49 per cent. 51 per cent being aban
doned. Minneapolis wired sales of 40,000 bbls
Flour, of which two-thirds was for export.
Philadelphia reported Continental acceptances
Of Wheat at Is- advance. Liverpool reported
sales of twelve cargoes off coast since Monday.
Later on the feeling was easier, following a
decline at St. Louis. Foreigners were inclined
to cover and considerable July wheat ap
peared.
The local situation was unchanged. ' .

HAT AND FEEDSTUFFS.
There is a fair demand for â¢ Hay at prac
tically unchanged â  quotations. Receipts are .
light, but are expected to be heavy after the
Fourth-. * '-: '
The circular of Somers & Co. says: "We
have a very uninteresting market to report at
the present time, and we may reasonably ex
pect .but little to he done with hay for the
next week or ten days. Very little if any of
the new crop hay now being marketed has
been through the stack and much Is, there
fore, improperly cured. We are con
stantly hearing complaints from apparently
dry looking hay which proves to be oftentimes
sour and sometimes hot. Shippers of hay are
probably satisfied that theirs Is well cured he
fore marketing; same, but after being con
fined In a close car for a few days It com
mences to sweat in the bale. Consumers will
not buy this kind of hay to lay away; there
fore same has to be sold for Immediate con
sumption. Until we can be reasonably as
sured that everything marketed Is in good
shape and is fit to be stored away for winter's
use. we will have no well-established market
here. Much of the crop In the country Is now
In the stack, and the farmers are all busy har
vesting. When they are through . with â¢ their
grain they will prepare to market their hay.
and we may then expect free shipments of
well-cured hay on the San Francisco market.
"Receipts are not very heavy for this time
of year, and yet they are ample for all de
mands. Alfalfa is scarce and in good demand.
There is scarcely enough being marketed for
a well-established price."

THE STOCK MARKET.
Securities were quiet on the morning session
and fluctuations were few and narrow.
On the afternoon call Gas and Electric de
clined to $70 50, and Giant Powder advanced to
$73 25. â  ' I. ,â :_.
Mining stocks, were weak and lower in the
morning, but recovered in the afternoon,
though the best prices were below those of the
preceding day. The telegram from the pump
said: "In the last 48 hours the water has been
lowered a total of 12 feet. During each stop of
one hour and ten minutes the water rose 7
feet. At 6:30 a. m. the water was down 160
feet below the 1750 level. The elevator is run
ning steadily."
The Oceanic Steamship Company will pay the
usual 50c per share dividend on July 1.
The Boston & Colorado Smelting Company
has declared a regular quarterly dividend of Hi
per cent, payable July 1.
During the past sixty days the De Lamar
mine in Eastern Nevada has cleaned up $100,000.
; The Dexter mine nt Tuscarora, New, has
made another shipment of $4000 in bullion and
i auro-cyanides valued at $2000 to Salt Lake City.
The Savings and Loan Society has declared a
dividend of 3.54 per cent on term deposits and
3.20 per cent per annum on ordinary deposits,
the lowest ever paid. No more term deposits
will be received, as the . bank hereafter will
treat all deposits as ordinary.
The Hibernia Bank has declared a dividend
at the rate of 3 1-3 per cent per annum on all
deposits.

SUN. MOON AND TIDE
United States Coast and Geodetic Survey-
Times and Heights of High and Low
Waters at Fort Point, entrance to San
Francisco Bay. Published by official au
thortiy of the Superintendent.
NOTEâ The high and low waters occur at
the city front (Mission-street wharf) about
twenty-five minutep later than at Fort Point;
the height of tide Is the same it both places.
NOTEâ In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given In the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day In the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column (fives the second tide of
i the day, the third time column the third tide
and the last or right-hand column gives the
last tide of the day, except when there are but
; three tides, as sometimes occurs. The heights
| given are additions to the soundings on the
| United States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign (â ) precedes the height,
and then the number given is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference is the mean of the lower low water*.

AN OILY HABIT.
Th ' ! are people who become uncon-
sciously addicted to the butter habit;
that is to say, they like butter and
gradually eat a little more as the
months go by, until they find they have j
consumed, each meal, quite a quantity)
of fat. that they have not vitality
. . enough to properly digest
This indigested substance causes
â¢ more or less catarrh of the stomach

ANGELS RETIRE
AND
LAMBARDI REIGNS
Back to the Alhambra Go
the Italian Singers
Under Their Own Man
agement.
BY fISHTOI^ STEVENS
MOROSCO has rnme into hia
own and bo has T-ambardl.
The Lambarrlis are now
Lambardis Indeed, for the
good sipnor whose name they hear has
parted oompany with his Los Angeles
(translation: those angels) managers
and rented the Alhambra for a term of
days. Once again will these amiable
Italians pack up their clothes and
scenery and move int i the Eddy-street
theater. In the Alhambra to-night the
removal will be celebrated by a per
formance of "Mignon." So far the
Lambardi engagement has been a
hard-luck story, the result of wild man
agement. Good singers galore are in
the company. Such a bunch of princi
pals we never have heard outside of the
big organizations. Put the chorus is
bad, the orchestra worse, st.^.- man
agement still worse, and the leader
lacking in magnetism and authority.
We have been willing t<Â» applaud the
individual artist but not the ensemble.
The productions have been wanting in
distinction; they have i>>-.-n crude and
provincial. The company has needed a
manager, a weeder, a sorter, a chorus
master, a disciplinarian. Had the right
man with the right capital behind him
presented this company to San Fran
cisco there might have been another
story in the box-office. As it is, the
Lambardis have suffered pecuniary
disaster for which the town will be held
in blame by the musical enthusiasts.
The truth is there is too much opera
for San Francisco to support. Compe
tition in this line has not stimulated the
crowd, only bored It Walt a little while
and in the survival find the lit t â¦ - s t .
"Manon Lescaut" was the opera in
which the Lambardi company sang
farewell to the California last ni^ht.
A beautiful piece it is, Bcored in the
musical dialect of the day and full of
moving moments. In its class in
ferior only to the same composer's
"Boheme." How my colleagues fought
this opinion of mine when the two
were first produced here by Del Conte!
But time lias vouched the supremacy
of the "Boheme. '' "Manon" is ur
gently dramatic at times, Binashingly
orchestrated, but strenuous, too stren
uous on the whole, and too saccharine
in the last two acts. "Boheme" is a
masterpiece without an effort. But
why argue at this time of day?
"Manon" was very prettily sung and
very badly produced in the California
last night. The orchestra was better
than usual and still bad, the chorus
was the awful same. The principals
had the triumphs, which is all wrong
when Puccini writes the music and the
opera is the thing. Miss Sostegni was
almost thrilling in Manon; she missed
a lot of the part's caprice and humor
and she was anything but musical in
some of her top notes; but she gave
the character, a tremendously difficult
(.lit-, a fair Identity and easily merited
the shouts of approval that followed
her efforts in the passionate scenes.
Badaracco was exorbitantly good as
the Chevalier, much more successful
than AffOStinl of the Del Conte com
pany, for his voice was fresh and free
in the final scenes that always ex
hausted his predecessor in the part.
As the tenor role of "Boheme" belongs
to Agostini, so does this one in
"Manon" belong to Badaracco. The
rest of the cast was in no manner re
markable.
Want a Place on the Ticket.
The Social Ivabor party yesterday filed
with the Registrar of Elections a petition
for a place on the ticket at the coming
election.
The registration office will be open this
evening from 7:30 to 9:30. The Election
Commission will meet at B o"ciock to
night to examine citizens of the Twenty
eighth, Twenty-ninth and Thirty-fifth
districts as to their qualifications to serve
as election officers.

THE SAN FR AX CISCO CALL, THURSDAY, JUNE 29, 1899.
CHURCH BURNED
TO THE GROUND
AT HAYWARDS
Grass Fire Spreads to
the Building.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
SOS Broadway, June 28.
The Methodist Church at Haywarda was
sacrificed this afternoon to the desire to
have a glorious Fourth. Haywards is
preparing a great celebration for next.
Tuesday, and this afternoon some young
fellows were busy burning sections of dry
grass in different parts of town; this is
being done as a precautionary measure
against possible dangers from fire during
the celebration.
One of the largest of the grass plats is
on B street, near the Methodist Church.
The trrass was set on fire, and it was
thought a careful watch was being kept
to prevent it reaching any of the adjoin
ing buildings. The insidious flames, how
ever, crept along the undergrowth and
soon reached the church and burned their
way through the walls. The fire was not
discovered in the church until it hud a
good start, and the wood being so dry the
progress of the flames was very rapid.
The Volunteer Fire Department re
sponded to the call, but could do noth
ing, as the church burst into flames on
all sides. A stiff breeze ad. led to the dif
ficulties of the firemen. Bom* 1 of the
church furniture was saved, but the
building Is a total wreck. It was valued
at 12000, and was built only a few years
ago.
The question is being discussed as to
whether the church has not a claim
against thus..- who started the grass lire.
It Is not known whether they were em
ployed by the celebration committee Âr
whether they were acting independently.
SNOW ABOUT READY
TO APPOINT MILLER
COUNCTLMEN TAKE A HAND IN
THE DEAL.
Police Commissioners Defer Action on
Many Matters Till the New
Fiscal Year.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
r*"iS Broadway, June 2-.
Tho Board of \\ orka will prohaMy re

Snow has refused to be hurried. It now
looks as though the Mayor ia ready to
act.
At this morning's meeting Mr. Dow
moved that the Miller resolution be re
ferred back to the committee of the
whole, ami this was done. This means
that the Mayor is ready to reconsider his
former action on thÂ»Â» plan to support
Miller. There was a caucus of Council
men last evening for the purpose of try-
Ing t<> induce the Mayor to vote for
Miller. It ended satisfactorily, though
with nothing definite on the part of the
r. He has state,) positively that he
not be a puppet In the hands of any
political clique, and he Is taking his own
time.
On motion of the Mayor S. H. Hare
was appointed to keep In condition the In
tersection ot Seventh and Market Btreets.
Several of the Improvement clubs have
advocated the keeping of men at certain
points for the purposi of attending to the
Btreets continually, and this la the
move in the establishment of
"c. nters."
When the members met as Police ami j
Fire Commissioners they carried out the
mmendationa discussed in committee
; ly. M. Hallahan was appointed an
extraman on No. 5 engine; the appoint
ment of Charles Hammerton as detective
