MAY HAVE BEEN POISONED.
Henri Portier Drank Milk and Died
Four Hours Later.
Henri Portier, a wine merchant, resid
ing at 617Â»/2 Valencia street, died about 5
O'clock yesterday afternoon and his body
v.is take;] to the Morgue, where an au
topsy will he performed to-day to ascer
tain the cause of death. Portier was taken i
ill about 1 o'clock yesterday afternoon
and Dr. Austin was called in to attend
him. He gave the patient some medicine,
but the latter expired four hours after
ward.
B< tore he died Portier said that he ne
liev< >i himself to have been poisoned by
milk which he drank shortly before he
was taken ill. His wife says that two j
weeks ago she also had been seized with !
a serious illness after drinking milk from
the same dairy.
Dr. Austin states that he can find no
trace of poison and ascribes heart disease
as the cause of death. The case will be
investigated to-morrow.
HOW TO STOP HEMORRHAGES.
Professor , Senn Explains the Best
'â¢ â¢' Technical Methods.
The lecture-room of Cooper Medical Col
lege was filled last night with a select
gathering of ladies and gentlemen, mostly
interested in the medical profession, to
hear a lecture by Professor Nicholas Senn
of the Rush Medical College. Chicago, one
of the most eminent surgeons and path
ologlsts of the present day.
The subject was "Prophylactic Hemo
stasis." or in plain words, the best means
of stopping hemorrhages. The eminent ;
lecturer talked for over an hour and was j
listened to with the closest attention.
In Introducing his subject he said: "The
intelligent selection and employment of ap
propriate and efficient prophylactic meas
ures are as important and of as far-reach-
value in surgery- as tomedjclpe.'^and 1
he went on dividing it into different heads,
including elevation, elastic constriction,
the elastic constrictor and its application,
special localities for elastic constriction
as a prophylactic hemostatic, hip joint,
shoulder joint, head, manual compression
of the aorta, digital compression, prelim
inary ligation of arteries in their contin
uity, temporary ligation of arteries, per
cutaneous temporary ligation of arteries
and veins, galvano and thermo earner and
angiotripsy.
At the conclusion of the lecture a large
number of physicians were introduced to
the lecturer by Professor Lane, and the
hope was expressed that he would permit
them to see him operate before taking his
departure from the city.
HEROIC RESCUE OF
A BRAVE YOUNG WOMAN
BOLiINAS, June 2S.â That Miss Mary
Stephens, a handsome young lady who
Is spending the summer, here, did not lose
her life in the treacherous waters of Bo
llnas Bay yesterday was due solely to her
presence of mind. Her rescue from a
perilous position was barely effected, but
without the coolness she herself mani
fested no rescue could have been possi
ble.
Miss Stephens is a girl of splendid phy
sique, an excellent swimmer, and fre
quently while bathing goes a good dis
tanve out into the surf. Yesterday after
noon shf swam as far out as the channel
and floated with the current.
When she started to return she found
that she had made no allowance for. the
strong tide running, and battle as she
would, she was slowly swept out toward
the ocean. As her strength was fast fall
ing she ceased to struggle, and, turning
on lier back, floated along crying for help.
Her shouts w.re heard by Charles Cline.a
Mill Valley painter, who rushed into the
surf, but could not overtake the girl.
Walking back to the shore, Cline ran
along the beach to a point 2(JO yards ahead
of Mis- Stephens and swam out in time
to reach her as she was being swept
across the bar. After a struggle lasting
ten minutes the brave rescuer reached
shore with his almost unconscious burden,
and sank exhausted on the sand.
The heroic act was witnessed by a large
crowd, who cheered heartily when both
were safe.
COLLIS P. HUNTINGTON
AS A PHILANTHROPIST
NEW YORK, June 28.â Collis P. Hunt-
Ington has undertaken another philan
thropic enterprise in the South. Within
the last fortnight he has purchased in
Hanover County, Virginia, some little,
distance above Richmond, a tract of 135>)
acres, on which he will have erected the
first reformatory for negro children In
Virginia. Save for an institution at Bir
mingham. Ala., t he entire South is said
to be barren of such institutions, and the
thousands of a race that furnishes more
than its proportion of offenders to the
Jails and penitentiaries are being cor
rupted by the contact and influence of
hardened adult negro criminals of both
Bexes.
The land lies along the Pamunk^v
River and adjoins a pk>t of about W0
acres already owned by the reformatory
association. Huntington Is careful, how
ever, to retain the title to the land "un
til such time as the institution appears
to t>e on a correel basis."

A NEW THEATER
TO BE ERECTED
FOR BELASCO
In the Heart of the
City.
Manager Belasco of the Alcazar Is to
have a new theater. He said last night
that he had signed an agreement with a
prominent business man for the erection
of a handsome building, up-to-date in
every particular, in which would be dedi
cated a grand temple of the drama.
"Last week," said Mr. Belasco, "I re
newed my lease on the Alcazar for three
years, beginning the first of August. At
its expiration I will go into a new theater,
which is to be erected for me. It will
be within a block of my present location.
I have inspected the site and looked over
the general plans. The theater will be
ready for occupancy the minute the Alca
zar lease expires. I have signed an agree
ment to that effect with a prominent busi
ness man of this city, whose name is a
sufficient guarantee that he means busi
ness. In addition to the theater the build
ing will contain a large number of lodge
rooms, and wtll be a handsome addition
to the architectural beauties of the city."
In speaking of the renewal of the lease
of the Alcazar, Mr. Belasco confirmed the
report that M. H. dc Young had doubled
the rental. In almost the same breath
he denied the equally nersistent rumor
that the hand of S. H. Frledlander of the
California Theater could be seen In the
Increase.
"If I thought that was so," he said;
"well, I will not say what I'd do. But
it isn't true."
The rumor was to the effect that Belasco
and Frledlander. who do not speak us
they jmss by, were collaborating well if
unwillingly in a new theatrical comedy
with the old title', "He Laughs Best That
Laughs Last." The first act was pre
sented to the public several weeks ago,
and made an instantaneous hit.- In it
Frledlander was discovered making his
exit P. D. Q., from the Alcazar. Belasco,
in a" towering rage, following, intent on
planting patent leather where it would
find the softest mark on the fugitive's
rear view. The prologue, unseen by the
audience, showed to the satisfaction of
Belasco that Frledlander had sought to
engage two youthful stars at that time
touring in the interior with the Belasco
& Thall company. A slap in the face,
according to all accounts, Is said to have
hastened Frledlander*s exit from the pain
fulness of the prologue Into the presence
of the public in the tirst act. The curtain
on that part of the comedy went down on
a rapidly lessening perspective of Fried
lander's coattailÃ as he outfooted the
portly Belasco.
A period of six weeks, filled by Fried
lander with a cram of Machiavelli, is sup-,
posed to have elapsed between the first
and second acts, in which M. H. de Young
a]. pears for the first time as a star come
dian. M. H. is discovered in his office,
softly cussing the Geary-street turntable,
when enters Friedlander, bearing a silver
platter, on which he makes an offer for
the Alcazar somewhat in excess of the
rental paid by Belasco.
"Hum," cou-ghed the director general,
eying it.
Exit Friedlander. chuckling. De Young.
solus, at the 'phone a minute later. "That
you, Fred? Yes? This is De Young.
I'll have to raise your rent. Got a dozen
offers for the theater. Yes. Just make it
double. Yes. 1 said a dozen. You know
me. You do. Well, that goes." Hangs up
the telephone, and scene changes to the
swearing-room in the Alcazar, where Bel
asco is making rasping noises with his
mouth as a curtain rolls up, hack, show
ing Friedlander in a blue light on a fence
smiling sardonically at his vanquished
enemy.
None of the principals, however, will
father this act. Belasco save he does not
believe it and Friedlander says he never
made an offer fur the Alcazar. The only
grain of truth, therefore, that seems to
be in the rumor lies in the fact that Bel
asco's rent has been doubled, and there's
no comedy in that, except tor De Young.

GENERAL EAGAN REALIZES
A FORTUNE IN HAWAII
s He Sells Uncultivated
Land Bought for a
Song at $100
Per Acre.
THE Roderick Dhu brought up
a lively budget of news from
the Hawaiian Islands yester
day. There is a boom in sugar and
sugar lands there. Coffee planta
tions are being torn up and sugar
e.me planted. Land that was pur
chased from the Government for
$3 an acre is selling at from $80
to SI SO an acre. A railroad to
corrxicet the various sugar indus
tries is being built. Major Gen
eral Egan, who went to Hilo pre
pared to "haul his son over the
coals'' for selling the family cof
fee plantation, gave the young
man his paternal blessing in
stead. The son had sold the un
cultivated portion of the land,
which rost his father $3 an acre,
for SIOO an acre, and the family
still possesses the 200 acres un
der cultivation. There is no
eruption of the volcano, but there
was a cave in of the ''pit," and
for several days the people of
Hawaii had "great hopes." The
most valuable collection of bird
skins ever made in Hawaii came
up from Hilo on the Roderick
Dhu. The collection is practically
priceless. It is consigned to the
National Museum in Washington.
Several planters who sold their
holdings for fortunes came up on
the Roderick Dhu.

the islands yesterday. They all made

DODGE ADDS
TWO MILLIONS
TO THE ROLLS
Corporations to Pay
Franchise Tax.
Assessor Do<lge yesterday added over
two million to his list of taxable values |
and he performed this praiseworthy feat I
without turning, a although there is
liable to be a great deal of perspiring on :
the part of those who will be called upon
to pay the taxes on this big sum. Re- j
cently Assessor Dodge received notice ;
from the Assessors of Alameda and San j
Joaquln. counties that certain corporations |
doing business in those counties refuse j
to p.ay a franchise tax on the ground that ,
they were incorporated in San Francisco
and the tax could be levied only In this
county under the law.
The companies referred to are the re
cently consolidated water companies of
Oakland and the Stockton Gas Company.
These companies claim San Francisco as
their home, and while they have never ;
paid any franchise litre they manage to j
escape the tax in the counties where they |
Â«lo .business on this ground alone. The
Assessor of s.i Joaquin County recently
brought suit to compel the Stockton Gas
Company to pay a franchise tax, and the,
answer ot the defendant sets up that such
a tax can only be collected In the county
of San- Francisco. Mr. Dodge proposes to :
Bee that these taxes are paid.
The Spring Valley "Water Company will
also be called upon to- pay taxes on an
additional JIw.OOO. The fact was brought
to the attention of the Assessor yesterday
that the water- company had never been
a-s=rs<sed for the reservoir known as the
JJernal Heights reservoir, located near
Thirtieth street and the old Mission road.
The reservoir was promptly assessed at
the rate of $f>o,ooO valuation for two years,
last ear and this year.
Another matter of importance that has
been acted upon by the Assessor is the
wiping out "i all assessments on personal
property which is valued at less than $50. â 
Hlfl reason for doing this is that it costs
the city more to collect these taxes than
It receives Consequently, in all cases
lierenftrr where the personal property tax ;
amounts to less than 51 the tax will be 'â 
remitted, this being, regarded by the As
sessor as \ more economical than paying.
52 or $3 to â -â â  -i the money into the treasury.
Fully 3000 families are benefited by this
action on the part of the ctly, and the
Assessor, and the city is none the loser,
as the 1120,000 which these poor people
have heretofore paid Into the treasury
is more than covered In. three separate
instances by the increase in the assess
ment of commercial firmsâ increases
which have in every instances been ac
i epted without a- murmur of protest. Dr.
Dodge Bays the. city really loses- money
by collecting taxes where the amount
falls below $4. as the cost is more than
the amount turned into the treasury.

MINNIE ADAMS' TRIAL.
Prosecution Introduces Very Tame
Testimony Against the Alleged
Murderess.
The evidence against Mrs. Minnie !
Adams, accused of the murder of her In- |
fant son, John Richard Gray, is now ,
purely circumstantial. Up to date not a I
bit of direct evidence has been produced j
against the accustyl and the prosecution
is apparently becoming desperate, as
every move In the trial is warmly con
tested.
Yesterday various witnesses were
placed upon 1 the stand, but their evidence
did little toward strengthening the theory
of the prosecution. J. J. FTannagan, t<
deputy employed in the Coroner's office,
testified to finding a piece of paper in
the out building at Mrs. Adams' honjQ
which bore the odor of carbolic acid. It
is claimed that the woman took the child
into the building and held it on her knee
while she poured the acid down its throat.
Mrs. Adams claims that the child had
found a bottle of carbolic acid in the
yard and had drank it.
Frank T. Green, chemist of the Board
of Health, testified that the carbolic add
from which the child met its death was
poison and gave considerable technical
testimony.
Detective Graham and Police Officers
Armstrong and J. P. Hurlihy also testi
lied. Hurlihy was called to Identify the
accused as' a woman who had purchased
carbolic acid in Loehr's drug stort at the
corner of Geary and Devisadero streets
the night preceding the murder. When
Hurllhy left the stand the case went over
until this morning.
DESERTERS DROWNED.
Japanese Sailors Lose Their Lives At
tempting to Escape From
Quarantine.
The bodies of two Japanese sailors wore
found on the t>each near Fort Point yes
terday foreno/m tied together with baling
rope. They each wore a life preserver
marked "Nippon Main," and were evi
dently members of the crew of th.it ves
sel, which Is detained in quarantine, and
had been drowned while attempting to
escape.
Coroner Hill, assisted by Drs. Zabala
and Lawlor, performed an autopsy at the
Morgue. No evidences of disease were
discovered except a slightly swollen condi
tion of the axillary glands cm one of the
bodies, and in order to prevent the possi
bility of the bubonic plague being Intro
duced into this port the bodies were im
mediately consumed at the Odd Fellows'
Crematory.

"There has been no eruption of the

Santiago, now daily expected, there
is only one spare berth left.
The Roderick Dhu brought up one

It is consigned to the National
Museum at Washington and has taken
I 3SOI Henshaw nearly five years
to gather. Every bird that makes its

sented, and the collection is almost
priceless. It is packed with the
greatest care, and will go East by ex
press. It was watched as carefully
during the passage as it would havo
been had it been specie.

art-streel wharf and there was no means
of keeping the soldiers on the vessel after
they had been put aboard. In consequence
a number of them made their escape to
the neighboring saloons and when the
transport sailed a dozen of them were left
behind. An attempt was made to stop the
vessel at Meiggs wharf, but it failed, so
the deserters will be sent to the Philip
pine" on the Pennsylvania to-morrow.
The navy transport Solace will sail for
Manila Saturday. She will carry forty
seven officers f"r the fleet in Manila Bay.
Lieutenant Cooper, with a draft of naval
apprentices, arrived from the Bast yes
terday. They also will go to Manila on
the Solace.
Captain F. T. Henry of the American
ship Cyrus Waketield is dead. A cable to
the Merchants' Kxchange states that the
\Yak< tield put into Port Stanley, Falkland
Islands, on June IS, with the captain's
body. The vessel was slightly damaged
in a storm, but whether Captain Henry
was killed during the gale or died from
natural causes is not known.
The deceased's family lives at 708 Golden
Gate avenue. Captain Henry was one of
the best known masters that came to this
port. He had been in the trade between
.New York and San Francisco for years
and was on his way here from the East
: when be died.
The red sta.k tug Redmond was towed
over from Oakland Creek yesterday. She
was taken over there to get a piece of
chain out of her wheel, but when she
was beached she filled with water and re
fused to float until she was pumped out.
The Redmond is now all right and will
be in commission again in a day or so.
The British ship Casswell is now over
due at Guayaquil from Newcastle, N. S.
\V.. and f>o per cent reinsurance is being
paid on her. She Is now out 131 days.

WORTHY OF ASSISTANCE.
Sad Case of Margaret Finney, a Dis-
charged Insane Patient.
A woman named Margaret Finney, who
has been in the Napa Hospital for the In
sane for the last twelve years, was dis
( harped as sane on Friday, June 23. She
was put on the boat, and on arriving in
this city Immediately went to British
Consul General Pickersglll and made a
pitiful appeal for assistance.
It appears that the asylum authorities
had sent her away without making any
provision for her future. She did not have
a cent of money, was without relatives or
friends and her clothes were in rags.
Consul Pickersgill had her taken to the
office of the British Benevolent Society,
which took steps to have the unfortunate
woman taken care of. She is perfectly
rational now. The society paid for a
week's room and board at the house of
Mrs. Wright, corner of Folsom and Sixth
streets;,, and that will expire to-night. She
will be cared for, however, until she can
obtain a position.
I. P. Nieto, chairman of the board of
relief of the benevolent association, In dis
cussing the case yesterday, said:
"Miss Finney is a most deserving
woman, and I am sure that if some ladies'
aid society were to take her In hand
she would appreciate it and be in every
way worthy of it. She is a native of
Kdinburgh, 32 years of age, and was com
mitted to the asylum in 1887 while she
was in the employ of Rev. Dr. Mackenzie.
She has never been violent, her dementia
being of a mild form. Our society will
not desert her in her trouble, but it seems
to me that it is a case for some ladies to
take up.
"I must say it is a shame that the asy
lum authorities should turn a poor woman
adrift without giving her some means to
get along for a short time, at least. Why,
convicts are given a suit of clothes and $5
when they are discharged from prison.
Dr. Mackenzie is out of town at present,
but I have no doubt that he would inter
est himself in the case if he were here."

ST. BRENDAN'S CHURCH
SERIES OF BENEFITS BY ST. AN
THONY'S BOOTH.
Efforts to Lift Indebtedness of tho
Congregation â Parishioners to

The first entertainment and social In
' aid of St. Brendan's fair was given by
! the members of St. Anthony's booth last
night at St. Brendan's Hall. -The proceeds
of the fair, which Is to be held next Sep
tember, will be devoted to paying off the
indebtedness incurred by the church and
the various booths will each present an
entertainment preparatory to the holding
of the fair.
Last night the hall was well filled with
an appreciative audience and an excel
lent programme was rendered by the
young people of the parish, aa follows:
Overture. Koesel's Orchestra; remarks, Rev.
I J. F. Nugent; solo, selected, Miss S. Hull;
i fancy dance, May Wholsky; comic song, J. B.

Walsh; recitation, selected. William Walsh;

men; piano solo, selected. Miss M. Stein; paro
dies. Max Stelnle.

U'Brien, was enjoyed by a large number.

Clark Gets a Stay.
Joseph Clark, -who was convicted nf
killing Fireman Joseph P. Gross and son
tenced to imprisonment for life In San
Quentln, Becured a stay of execution yes
terday just, aa the Sheriff was about to
s'-mi him across the bay. When he
learned of the Sheriffs intention his at
torney applied to the Superior Court for
a stay bo that the papers in the appeal
mijjfht ho prepared, but the trial Jinl^'
was off on his vacation, and the Judge
imsidinK temporarily on the bench re
fused to interfere In the matter at all.
Application was made to the Supreme
Court for a stay of execution, which, al
though a little informal, was granted,
and Clark will not go to the State Prison
until the case has been finally decider]
by the hlffhcr court.

THE SOCIALISTS.
Talks About the New Charter and Its
Benefits to the People of
the City.
President F. YV. Dohrmann of the Mer
chants' Association addressed Liberty
Branch, Socialist Labor party, it the
Turk-streeil Temple last night upon "Mu
nicipal Cuvfrnni'Mit." The speaker was
introduced bj Chairman C M. Bartleu.
Mr. Dohrmann took as the n.xt of hla
subject th< construction ril the new char
i. r in the practical relation that it will
bear to the welfare of the people, of tht
city. He spoke In a plain and practical
manner, giving his personal views of thj
i.. neflts and disadvantages that confront
people of any city as a reason of ih â 
charter under which y. municipal govern
ment is operated; He dwelt forcibly up
on tho fact that the charter of a city,
affecting as it does every municipal in
terest, is the vital thing for The peop
a city to ensider. He said thai self-gov
ernment has not been a predominating
feature in San Francisco for forty years.
The spoils system, lie declared; must I>>
abolished and the civil service, which i 3
embodied in the new charter, substituted,
Mr. Dohrmann then went Into a gen
eral discussion of the affairs of munici
pal government. Dr. J. S. Scott and A.
Sorensen were the critics.

THE SA:N FRANCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, JUNE 29, 1899.
BARBAT HOLDS
THE EDGE ON
THE OTHERS
Health Board Is All
Split Up.
RIVALCOLLEGES RESPONSIBLE
A PRETTY FIGHT TO OUST DR. G.
E. SUSSDORF.
Keeney, Coffey and Payne Ready to
Do It, but Burns' Family Physi
cian Has an Ax to
Grind.
As pretty a fight as one would wish to
see in a. lifetime is now being waged in
the now Board of Health. On the surface
it appears to be Dr. Barbat against his
colleague?-! >rs. Payne, Coffey and Kee
iuy. As a matter of fact, it is the united
faculties df the Cooper and University of
California medical colleges against the
College of Physicians and Surgeons. In
the preliminary sparring so far the "P.
and 5.," as the Fourteenth-street insti
tution is called, has had all the advan
tage. Latterly, however, its united op
ponents have developed wonderful recu
perative powers and the contest promises
to go the limit to a draw.
In such an eVent one man will be happy.
He is Dr. <;. E. Sussdorf, the present
superintendent of the City and County
Hospital. The longer the fight lasts the
i r he will hold office, and should it
end up with no decision he will continue
in his place. Three members of the Board
of Health have intimated their wish to
create a vacancy by disposing of him. the
intention being to appoint as his suc
cessor Pr. David E. Hodghead, who has
an office In the McNuti Hospital on Sut
ter street and 1b the candidate of I>r.
Wlnslow Anderson, dean of the faculty
of the College of Physicians and Surgeons.
The fourth niemli. r of the board, Dr.
Barbat, Is perfectly willing to pet rid of
lorf. but refuses to budge in that
direction until he is absolutely certain
that !.â â¢ candidate of the "P. and S." will
get the place.
Barbat announced himself to that ef
fect a week ago last night. At a secret
meeting of the board to oil the toboggan
â¢ â¢ doomed employes of the Health
Departmeni it was proposed to oust Suss
dorf. Keeney, Coffey and Payne voted in
the affirmative; Barbat in the negative,
stating that he knew it to be the purpose
of his colleagues to elect Hodghead. He
said he had nothing persanalh against
Hodgnead, merely opposing: him as the
candida!. of a college. Dr. Barbat's stand
d it. It takes four votes to create a
vacancy, and only three to elect Without
1: :ii at's vote the other three had nothing
to nil, and there they stand and are likely
nd until Dan Hums cracks the whip
and makes his family physician hunt his
hole.
Had Dr. Barbat been candid with his
CoHeagues h< would have told them that
he opposed Hodghead not because h<- was
the representative of a college but be
cause he was the representative of the
College of Physicians and Surgeons
which is largely Dr. Winslow Anderson.
Fie could have told them also maybe th"y
know ii that the mere mention of the
name of Dr. Anderson in the presence of
Barbat Is like flaunting a red ran In front
"J a bull, ii makes Harbat bellow.
This may be because, while Hart. at Is
physician to the family of Dan Burns Dr
Anderson enjoys the full confidence of the
Mexican and has been called In on extra
ordinary occasions. It may he. that the
jaundice of professional jealousy has col
ored the feelings of the family physician
to Buch an extent that they Influence hi?
action In the Board of Health. However
that may be, it remains of record that
Bafbat, who agreed with bis colleagues
in putting a clothespin on the no.s<-> of thÂ«
Board of Health while the Hums off
scourings -were being passed into office,
does not agrei with them on the man to
be elected superintendent of the City and
County Hospital.
For the present Barbat Beetns to have
th^ whip hand: His colleagues have the
three votes necessary to elect Hodghead,
but not the four needful to oust Sussdorf.
The principal Interest th<> Cooper and
California college faculties have in the
fight is against further encroachment of
the College of Physicians and Surgeons
on what they have considered their par
ticular preserve. For that reason they
support Barbat in his opposition to Hodg
head. who rr presents the P. and fi.
They know that with Sussdorf out' of the
way. Dra Keeney, Coffey and Payne
would put In, If not Hodghead, some other
P. and S. candidate: The action of
ih^ three physicians at a meeting on tho
very night on which they first tried to
oust Sussdorf, Barbat showed that. Dr.
Payne introduced an Innocent looking res
olution to the effect that the College of
Physicians and Surgeons be allowed equal
privileges at the City and County Hospi
tal with the medical department of the
University of California and Cooper Med
ical College; also that it have the privi-
Lege of appointing one visiting physician
and one visiting Burgeon without pay;
and. further, to appointt an equal numbi r
Of graduates as internes.
The resolution was adopted with only
one dissenting vote (Barbat's)/ but the
fact that the triumph was not without op
position made it none the less bitter for
the opponents of the College of Physicians
and Surgeons, which had at lÃst won Its
long fight and was finally on equal foot
ing wiih the others.
Wiih Sussdorf out and Hodghead in the
I", and S. would be cock of the walk, with
the . others nowhere. It remains to be
seen whether or not Barbat can hold the
fort.

GONE TO HER REST.
The Passing Away of Mrs. Lillian A. ;
Carlie, a Prominent Member of
the Native Daughters.
A wreath of beautiful flowers and along
streamer of purple ribbon hung: on the
: door of the Native Daughters' Home yes
terday, an emblem of sorrow for "the
; death of one of the prominent members of
; the order, of Native Daughters of the
I Golden West, Mrs.. Lillian. A. Carlle. The
. deceased, who was a sister, of the late
Frank J. Higgins. the first president of
the Grand. Parlor of the Native Sons of
the Golden West; had . for a Jong time i
prior to her demise been a great sufferer ;
; from a disease, which she knew was in
curable, Despite her Illness, she was an
ardent worker;, for the advancement of
: the order, which, 1 next to her. only son,
: she loved with an ardor, that has seldom,
if .ever, been equaled by any one in that
.organization,. and so long as she was able <
to attend the meetings of La Estrella. j
Parlor, of which she was the founder, |
she, was present to assist in the upbuild- j
ing thereof. -A' few weeks ago she at- |
tended a social function given by ; her par- ;
lor and to an intimate friend she said.;. '
"This, ;i. feel, will be the last time that I
.will see my girLs enjoy themselves. I al
ways call them 'ray girls,' for I. am their
; t'rat'-rnal mother. I feel that I am soon
to go. and while I hate to leave my darl- |
ing son and : these lovable girls, . I am j
ready -when the summons comes. 1 " A few ;
days after that she became. so ill that she
was taken to the home, where at 5. .o'clock
on last Tuesday afternoon she -peacefully
passed away. \ .., â : â  â  .; . .':' : .â¢ '
Mrs. I'arlie was an unusually ' bright
'â  woman, highly educated; one of high ar- :
tistic taste, a fluent writer and possessed
of fine voice,' which she frequently' used 1
to charm the members of the parlors. She
â was a regular attendant at all the grand i
parlor sessions;' and. being well versed in
legislation rendered valuable assistance in
framing laws for the order. She was the
composer of one of; the odes now in use,
and for which she was awarded, a prize. ]
i But owing to her illness her absence at
the last held Grand Parlor.was noted, and
many were: the words of sympathy ex
pressed.- For several terms she was one
of the grand trustees of tho Grand Parlor. .
Shortly before her death she called to
â¢ her. bed.slde some of the sisters in th.3
I order and gave them directions as to how
her funeral should be conducted, and her
wishes will be carried out under the
auspices of the members of La Estrella
Parlor. She was a native of Columbia,
Tuolumhe. County, and was In the forty
third year of her age. The funeral will
be held from the home at 2 o'clock this |
afternoon.

To Lake Tahoe During the Holidays.
Another grand outing has just been
arranged for pleasure-seekers of the
bay rities by the. Southern Pacific
Company. On Friday, Saturday, Sun
day and Monday next, on train leaving
this city at 6 p. m., there will be Lake
Tahoe excursions, for which the ex
tremely low round-trip rate of Jll 30
has been made. A steamer trip around
charming Lake Tahoe and a chance to
visit any -of its delightful resorts is in
cluded. Tickets 1 will be good for return
on any regular train from Truckee up
to noon of Thursday, July 6, so that
ample time is given for a royal trip.
This is the very height of the Tahoe
season, and the- rate offered is unpreco
dentedly low. Tickets are now on sale,
either at the Hiy ticket office, 613 Mar
ket street, under the fJrand Hotel, -or at
the ferry depot. Don't lose a chance
like this. Remember the datesâ Friday
to Monday next, inclusive, on 6 p. m.
train. . â  .'
